5:33:30
anchors Amos and I once rigged A Wheel of Fortune it was marked from 0 to 100

5:33:44

5:33:57

5:34:03

5:34:09

5:34:15

5:34:22

5:34:27

5:34:35

5:34:41
45% respectively the phenomenon we were studying is so common and so important
5:34:48
in the everyday world that you should know its name it is an anchoring effect
5:34:55
it occurs when people consider a particular value for an unknown quantity
5:35:00

5:35:05
reliable and robust results of experimental psychology the estimates stay close to
5:35:12
the number that people considered hence the image of an anchor if you are asked whether Gandhi
5:35:20
was more than 114 years old when he died you will end up with a much higher
5:35:25

5:35:31
35 if you consider how much you should pay for a house you will be influenced
5:35:37
by the ass asking price the same house will appear more valuable if its listing
5:35:42
price is high than if it is low even if you are determined to resist the influence of this number and so on the
5:35:50

5:35:56

8:28:41

8:29:01

8:29:08

8:29:14

8:33:43

8:33:49

8:34:02

8:34:07

8:35:09

8:35:14

8:35:22

8:35:29

8:35:36

8:35:42

8:36:12

8:36:18

the correlation between self-reported height and weight among adult American males
8:36:32

8:36:37
because individuals gender and age influence both their height and their
8:36:43

8:36:50

8:36:56

1:28:01

1:28:22

1:28:36

1:28:41

1:28:56

1:29:03

1:29:10

1:29:17

1:29:24

1:29:38

1:29:44

1:30:17

1:30:31

1:30:37

1:39:44

1:40:17

1:40:23

1:40:29

1:41:06

1:41:13

1:41:21

1:42:54

1:52:07

1:52:14

1:52:19

1:52:24

1:52:32

1:52:37

1:52:43

1:52:49

1:52:55

1:53:01

1:53:06
22. Picture a seasoned stock broker sitting at his desk. Screens glow with

1:53:31

1:53:37

1:53:47

1:53:52

1:54:13

1:54:18

1:54:30

1:54:36

1:54:43

1:54:56

1:55:03

1:55:11

1:55:17

1:55:22

1:55:29

1:55:37

1:56:10

1:56:17

1:56:31

1:57:47

1:58:09
23. Think back to a shocking news event. An election upset. A company suddenly
1:58:14

1:58:41

1:58:46

1:58:53

1:59:01

1:59:15

1:59:20

1:59:25

1:59:30

1:59:38

1:59:43
Cannaman warns that hindsight bias is more than a curiosity. It shapes how we
1:59:48

1:59:54
all along, we underestimate how much luck, randomness, and complexity were at play, we grow overconfident in our
2:00:02

2:00:09

2:00:14

2:00:19

2:00:33

2:00:39

2:00:52

2:00:57

2:01:04

2:01:09

2:01:16

2:01:49

2:01:56

2:02:08

2:02:20

2:02:27

2:03:28

2:06:04

2:18:01
even when we neglect it. Part four, choices. Chapter 27. Imagine you're
2:18:06
offered a gamble, flip a coin. If it lands heads, you win $150. If tails, you
2:18:13
lose $100. Would you take it? Most people say no. Losing $100 feels worse
2:18:19
than gaining $150 feels good. That gut reaction reveals one of Conaman's most
2:18:24
famous ideas explored in chapter 27. Prospect theory and the principle of
2:18:29
loss aversion. Prospect theory was Conoran and Tverki's revolutionary alternative to the classic model of
2:18:35
human decision-making. Economists once believed people act rationally, maximizing expected utility. But in real
2:18:43
life, people don't weigh gains and losses equally. Losses hurt about twice as much as gains of the same size bring
2:18:50
joy. Think about finding $20 in your pocket. It's a nice little thrill. Now,
2:18:56
think about losing $20 unexpectedly. It stings more. This asymmetry shapes
2:19:01
countless choices we make. From gambling to investing to everyday spending. I felt this bias in my own life. Once I
2:19:10
sold an item online for less than I paid, even though I rarely used it. Rationally, I should have been happy to
2:19:16
have some money back. But emotionally, the loss compared to the original price gnawed at me. I wasn't evaluating the
2:19:23
present gain. I was anchored to the past cost. Conorman explains that prospect
2:19:28
theory maps how we value outcomes not in absolute terms but relative to a reference point. That point might be the
2:19:36
status quo or an expectation or even a price we paid. Gains feel good above it.
2:19:42
Losses feel terrible below it. This explains why people reject fair gambles.
2:19:47
A 50/50 chance to win $150 or lose $100 is mathematically favorable. The
2:19:53
expected value is positive. But because the pain of losing outweighs the pleasure of winning, most people walk
2:20:00
away. It also explains behaviors like selling winning stocks too early, locking in gains to savor them while
2:20:07
holding on to losing stocks too long, refusing to accept the pain of loss.
2:20:12
Investors aren't maximizing wealth, they're minimizing regret. Conorman illustrates how loss aversion affects
2:20:19
negotiations. A pay cut feels unbearable while a raise of the same size brings
2:20:25
only modest joy. That's why workers resist wage cuts fiercely even in
2:20:30
recessions. Employers aware of this are often forced to lay people off instead
2:20:36
because spreading small losses evenly feels worse than imposing larger ones on
2:20:41
a few. I find this fascinating in everyday contexts. Consider shopping. If
2:20:47
an item is on sale from $100 to $70, it feels like a gain. Even if the item was
2:20:53
never really worth $100, the original price sets a reference point, framing
2:20:59
the discount as a victory. Marketers exploit our sensitivity to loss and gain
2:21:04
constantly. Prospect theory also reshapes how we think about risk. People are risk averse when it comes to gains,
2:21:12
preferring a sure $100 over a 50% chance at $200. but risk-seeking when facing
2:21:18
losses, preferring a 50% chance of losing $200 over a sure loss of $100.
2:21:25

2:21:31

2:21:39

2:22:09

2:22:16

2:22:21

2:23:23

2:23:30

2:23:36

2:23:45

2:23:51

2:23:57
28. The four-fold pattern of risk attitudes revealed by prospect theory.
2:24:02
Here's the pattern. High probability of gain. Risk averse. We prefer a sure win
2:24:08
over a gamble, even if the gamble has higher expected value. That's why people take $900 for sure instead of risking
2:24:15
for $1,000. High probability of loss. Risk-seeking. We prefer to gamble to
2:24:21
avoid a sure loss, even when the gamble is worse mathematically. That's why people risk losing $1,000 instead of
2:24:28
accepting a certain $900 loss. Low probability of gain, risk-seeking. We
2:24:34
love long shots like lotteryies. A tiny chance at a big gain excites us far more
2:24:39
than a small sure gain. Low probability of loss. Risk averse. We overpay to
2:24:44
eliminate tiny risks like buying insurance against rare disasters. Put together, this four-fold pattern
2:24:51
explains a huge swath of human behavior that classical economics can't. Think about lotteryies. Rationally, they're
2:24:58
terrible bets. The odds are microscopic and the expected value is negative. Yet,
2:25:03

2:25:08

2:25:14
extended warranties or flight insurance rarely makes sense. But people do it to eliminate even tiny risks. That's the
2:25:22
fourth quadrant. Risk averse when the probability of loss is small. I can see
2:25:27

2:25:34

2:25:40

2:25:47

2:25:52
probability losses, the hope of escape tempts us to gamble. With small chances,
2:25:57

2:26:04
this explains the paradox of people buying both lottery tickets and insurance. On the surface, it's
2:26:11

2:26:19

2:26:26

2:26:33

2:26:46

2:26:51

2:26:59

2:27:06

2:40:17

2:50:11

2:50:17

2:50:22
insurance policy, investment, I ask what policy makes sense over dozens of
2:50:28
similar choices. That perspective makes it easier to say no to gamles that feel tempting but don't pay off in the long
2:50:35
run. Conoran also points out that organizations can protect themselves from bias by setting clear policies. For
2:50:42
instance, a company might adopt a rule, we don't invest in projects unless expected returns exceed 15%. That rule
2:50:49
keeps them from being swayed by enthusiasm for particular ventures. Individuals can do the same. I don't buy
2:50:56
lottery tickets. I don't chase sunk costs. Policies create consistency where
2:51:01
emotions create chaos. What fascinates me is how much discipline this requires.
2:51:07
System one lives in the moment, reacting to each gamble, each loss, each thrill.
2:51:13
System two has to step in, designing policies and enforcing them. It's like building guard rails against your own
2:51:20
psychology. But there's a payoff. Broad framing and risk policies don't just
2:51:25
reduce losses, they reduce regret. When you follow a consistent rule, you may
2:51:31
still lose sometimes, but you know the decision was part of a sound policy that
2:51:36
makes the sting easier to bear. Personally, this chapter makes me rethink how I handle risks. Instead of
2:51:43
asking what feels right now, I try to ask what policy would I want to follow
2:51:48
every time. That small shift turns emotional choices into strategic ones.
2:51:53
Chapter 33. Leaves us with a practical insight. We can't eliminate our biases,
2:52:00
but we can outsmart them with rules. By framing decisions broadly, by thinking
2:52:05
in terms of repeated games instead of one-off gambles, we can make choices that serve us better over time. So the
2:52:12
next time you're stuck on a risky choice, pause. Don't just ask, "Do I want this gamble?" Ask, "What policy
2:52:20
would I want for all similar gamles?" Because in the end, wisdom isn't about winning one toss. It's about playing the
2:52:27
whole game well. Chapter 34. Imagine you're standing in front of two doors.
2:52:33
Behind one, a guaranteed $500. Behind the other, a 1% chance to win $50,000.
2:52:42
Which do you choose? For some, the certainty of $500 feels safe and
2:52:47
comforting. For others, the tiny chance of hitting it big sparks excitement.
2:52:52
Both choices reveal how we distort probabilities when making decisions. In chapter 34, Conaman ties together the
2:53:00
threads of prospect theory, loss aversion, the four-fold pattern, the certainty effect, the possibility effect
2:53:07
into a fuller picture of how we actually handle risk. He calls it the prospect
2:53:12
theory value function. Here's the gist. We don't see outcomes in absolute terms.
2:53:18
We judge them relative to a reference point. Gains bring joy, losses bring
2:53:24
pain, and losses loom larger than gains. But probabilities, we don't see them
2:53:29
clearly either. We overweight small probabilities and underweight moderate and high probabilities. That's why
2:53:35
lotteryies thrive and insurance sells. The chance of winning a jackpot is tiny, but our minds inflate it. The chance of
2:53:43
a rare disaster is tiny, but we inflate that, too. Meanwhile, the difference between 60% and 70% odds hardly
2:53:51
registers emotionally, even though mathematically it's significant. I see this bias every time I buy a raffle
2:53:57
ticket. I know the odds are slim, but I catch myself daydreaming about winning.
2:54:03
The emotional weight of possibility overshadows probability. On the flip side, when I buy travel insurance, I
2:54:10
know the risk is low, but I pay anyway. The peace of mind feels worth it. Conoran points out that this distorted
2:54:16
waiting makes us inconsistent. We shy away from gamles with high probabilities of gain, risk averse at the top end, but
2:54:24
we chase gamles with low probabilities of gain, risk-seeking at the bottom. We
2:54:30
refuse sure small losses, risk-seeking for high probability losses, yet we
2:54:35
overpay to eliminate tiny losses, risk averse for low probability losses. It's
2:54:41
not logical, but it's deeply human. One striking example he gives is how people respond to medical treatments. If a drug
2:54:48
offers a small chance of preventing a deadly disease, patients often cling to it, even if the chance is minuscule.
2:54:55
Hope magnifies the possibility. On the other hand, if a drug almost always
2:55:01
works but has a small chance of failure, patients may reject it. Fear magnifies
2:55:07
the risk. Same math, different emotional weights. What fascinates me is how this plays out in public life. Politicians
2:55:14
exploit it when framing policies. Want to pass a law? Emphasize the tiny risk
2:55:20
it prevents. Want to sell a dream? Emphasize the tiny chance of glory.
2:55:25
People respond not to the numbers, but to the emotional punch of possibility or certainty. Personally, this chapter
2:55:32
makes me rethink how I see risk. When I hesitate to take a good bet, I ask, "Am
2:55:37
I underweaiting a high probability?" When I get excited about a long shot, I ask, "Am I overweighting a small
2:55:44
probability?" It doesn't stop the bias, but it helps me check it. Conoran also shows how prospect theory explains why
2:55:51
people buy both lottery tickets and insurance. At first, it looks contradictory. Why would the same person
2:55:58
chase risk in one case and avoid it in another? But through the lens of distorted probability waiting, it makes
2:56:04
sense. Both are emotional purchases, not rational ones. What I love about this
2:56:10
chapter is how it reframes irrationality. We're not foolish for dreaming or
2:56:15
fearing. We're wired that way. Our minds evolved to overreact to rare events
2:56:22
because in the wild, rare dangers and rare opportunities could be life or death. Modern life turns those instincts
2:56:29
into quirks that markets and policies can exploit. The takeaway is not to suppress emotion, but to see it clearly.
2:56:37
If I buy a lottery ticket, I admit I'm buying hope, not investment. If I buy
2:56:42
insurance, I admit I'm buying peace of mind, not just value. And when I make big decisions, I remind myself that my
2:56:49
gut may be bending probabilities into shapes that don't match reality. Chapter 34 wraps up part 4 with a humbling
2:56:57
insight. Our choices aren't guided by cold logic. They're guided by distorted
2:57:03
perceptions of gain, loss, and probability. We are creatures of stories
2:57:08
and emotions, not calculators of odds. So the next time you're tempted by a long shot or reassured by certainty,
2:57:15
pause. Ask, "Is this about probability or possibility, about value or peace of
2:57:22
mind?" Because in the end, understanding our biases doesn't erase them, but it
2:57:28
does give us the power to navigate them with a little more wisdom. Part five,
2:57:33
two selves. Chapter 35. Close your eyes and imagine a vacation. You're lying on
2:57:38
a beach, warm sun on your skin, waves lapping in the distance. You feel
2:57:44
completely at peace. Now imagine that at the end of the trip, all your photos will be deleted. Your memory of the
2:57:51
experience wiped clean and no one will ever know you went. Would you still take
2:57:56
the vacation? This strange thought experiment opens the door to chapter 35 where Conaman introduces us to the two
2:58:03
selves inside each of us. The experiencing self and the remembering self. The experiencing self lives in the
2:58:11
moment. It feels the warmth of the sun, the sweetness of dessert, the sting of
2:58:16
disappointment. It experiences life second by second, minute by minute. The
2:58:22
remembering self, on the other hand, is the storyteller. It looks back, weaves
2:58:27
moments into a narrative, and decides whether an experience was good or bad.
2:58:32
Here's the twist. The remembering self usually dominates. It's the one that
2:58:37
keeps score, makes decisions, and tells us who we are. The experiencing self may
2:58:43
live the moments, but the remembering self decides which moments matter. Conorman illustrates this with a study
2:58:49
on pain. Patients underwent a medical procedure that was uncomfortable. Some
2:58:55
had shorter but more intense pain. Others had longer procedures with milder endings. When asked afterward, patients
2:59:02
judged their overall experience not by the total amount of pain, but by the peak, the worst moment, and the end. A
2:59:11
long but gentle ending left a better memory than a short sharp finish. This became known as the peak end rule. Our
2:59:18
remembering self doesn't tally every second. It highlights the highs and lows, especially the ending. The
2:59:25
duration hardly matters. That's why a twoe vacation can feel no more memorable than a oneweek vacation. The remembering
2:59:33
self condenses the story, focusing on peaks and how it ended. I felt this in
2:59:38
my own life. I once attended a concert where the music was amazing, but the last 20 minutes dragged with technical
2:59:44
issues. When I think back, that clumsy ending colors the whole night. My
2:59:51
experiencing self had hours of joy. My remembering self carries a story of disappointment. Conaman pushes us to
2:59:58
realize that these two selves often want different things. The experiencing self
3:00:03
wants pleasure, comfort, flow. The remembering self wants stories, meaning,
3:00:09
milestones. Sometimes they align, but often they conflict. Think about
3:00:15
students pulling all-nighters to pass exams. The experiencing self suffers. Tired, stressed, unhappy. But the
3:00:23
remembering self says, "It was worth it. I succeeded." Or think of marathons. The
3:00:30
experiencing self feels pain, exhaustion. The remembering self later
3:00:35

3:00:41
should we listen to when making decisions? If you plan a vacation, are you planning for the moments you'll live
3:00:47

3:00:53

3:00:58
others. Personally, I find this idea both unsettling and liberating.
3:01:04

3:01:11

3:01:16

3:01:24

3:01:30

3:01:36

3:01:44

3:01:50

3:01:56

3:02:02

3:02:07

3:02:15

3:02:22

3:02:27

3:02:32

3:02:40

3:02:46

3:02:51

3:02:58
chapter 36. Conorman explains that our memories of experiences aren't faithful recordings. They're summaries built from
3:03:06

3:03:11

3:03:16

3:03:22

3:03:28

3:03:35

3:03:41

3:03:48
for transport soured the memory. My remembering self filed the day as
3:03:53

3:03:58
This disconnect has big consequences. For one, it means our decisions about the future are often guided by
3:04:05

3:04:10
highlights and endings, we may choose to repeat it, even if in the moment much of
3:04:16

3:04:22
repeat two medical procedures. One shorter but ending painfully, the other longer but ending more gently.
3:04:29
Rationally, the shorter procedure meant less total suffering. But most patients preferred to repeat the longer one
3:04:36
because their remembering selves judged it more favorably. It's unsettling when you think about it. We don't choose
3:04:42
based on how much we actually suffer or enjoy. We choose based on how much we remember suffering or enjoying. The
3:04:49
remembering self steers the ship even if the experiencing self bore the weight. This raises an uncomfortable question
3:04:57
whose happiness really matters. The experiencing self that lives every moment or the remembering self that
3:05:03
writes the story. If your experiencing self is miserable during a marathon but
3:05:08

3:05:15

3:05:22

3:05:27

3:05:34

3:05:42
the hours of pain or joy. The remembering self discards that focusing
3:05:48

3:05:53

3:06:17

3:06:22

3:06:35

3:06:42

3:07:32

3:07:38

3:07:45

3:07:50

3:07:55

3:08:02

3:08:08

3:08:13

3:08:25

3:08:31

3:08:37

3:08:44

3:08:49

3:08:56

3:09:03

3:09:10

3:09:16

3:09:22

3:09:27

3:09:35

3:09:43

3:09:48

3:10:10

3:10:16

3:10:23

3:10:30

3:10:36

3:10:44

3:10:49

3:10:55

3:11:00

3:11:13
